    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17467722402129737542"><i>Being & Nothingness</i> is without doubt one of the most significant philosophical books of the 20th century. The central work by one of the century's most influential thinkers, it altered the course of western philosophy. Its revolutionary approach challenged all previous assumptions about the individual's relationship with the world. Known as 'the Bible of existentialism', i</span>
  <span id="freeText17467722402129737542" style="display:none"><i>Being &amp; Nothingness</i> is without doubt one of the most significant philosophical books of the 20th century. The central work by one of the century's most influential thinkers, it altered the course of western philosophy. Its revolutionary approach challenged all previous assumptions about the individual's relationship with the world. Known as 'the Bible of existentialism', its impact on culture &amp; literature was immediate &amp; was felt worldwide, from the absurdist drama of Samuel Beckett to the soul-searching cries of the Beat poets.<br /><i>Being &amp; Nothingness</i> is one of those rare books whose influence has affected the mindset of subsequent generations. Seventy years after its 1st publication, its message remains as potent as ever--challenging readers to confront the fundamental dilemmas of human freedom, choice, responsibility &amp; action.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17467722402129737542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/320384-do-you-find-this-book-meaningful">Do you find this book meaningful?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_320384'><a id="like_count_community_question_320384" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/320384?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">6 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F10033.Being_and_Nothingness">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/320384-do-you-find-this-book-meaningful">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/320384-do-you-find-this-book-meaningful">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/20260095-kent-manthie"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1368302065i/20260095._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/20260095-kent-manthie">Kent Manthie</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I guess many intellectuals manqués feign an understanding or familiarity with NAUSEA; who knows for what reason that may be: perhaps someone within th<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I guess many intellectuals manqués feign an understanding or familiarity with NAUSEA; who knows for what reason that may be: perhaps someone within their circle of friends read it and has been making references to it, attempting to discuss it in, what may be a sincere manner, although, as sincere as he may be, he may or may not truly understand the existential preludes, between the lines, so to speak, describing certain "scenes"/dialogue from the book, attempting to deconstruct NAUSEA; noting that the description of this or that incident or pontificating on possible metaphors, claiming to "know" what this or that might symbolize, etc. (though the "deconstruction" or the "knowing" that this or that is symbolic of "A" or "Z" is almost always THEIR interpretations of whatever work about which they happen to be expostulating; even in cases where (and here I'm speaking about certain books in general, not specifically NAUSEA) the text that the author wrote had no such symbology or undertones of "x" or "y"; what's truly ironic is when someone looks really deeply into a work &amp; "discovers" that 'a'='b', or that δ is symbolic of ψ, when in reality the author had no such idea when writing it or was even trying to be allegorical or metaphorical. This type of "psychoanalysis" of books, essays, monographs, etc. a fervent reader may attribute to such a work is, more - or even mostly - off-the-mark, thus it becomes mostly a projection of one's own psychology &amp; any baggage that might carry. As for the work in question, NAUSEA, it can be seen as a great" application of Sartre's brand of (as he called it) "French, atheistic existentialism". As wonderful a story as it is (I've read it, at this point, about 4 times or so &amp; plan on reading it many more times), e.g., Sartre's novel which is a kind of practical application of his brand of existentialism: the more one reads it, the closer one reads it, one becomes more and more absorbed into Sartre's concepts of life, the oppressiveness of life, the ennui which is anyone's typical day-to-day routine(s), as well as Sartre's vehement insistence that the lone, mind-as-being, his exultation of the individual as against the madding crowd, the sycophants, cronies, indoctrinated from a young age, via school, shows us that to love solitude is not, as any who feel the need to fit in with the masses merely solipsistic and certainly not narcissistic, In myriad passages, "scenes" or soliloquies by a character, be he protagonist or peripheral character, the words which make up said passages, are used like a great painter's color wheel or the sculptor's use of clay or stone, whatever the medium, to lament man's alienation from the world, though most people mindlessly go about their daily rituals with no self-awareness of the bad or good among the society in which he exists, but now &amp; then a unique character shows up &amp; is not only quite self-aware, or "self-conscious" of his own existence, but is able to extrapolate many things from the words and/or deeds of others. Although NAUSEA is a brilliant work, a book which any person who yearns to find some connection with the alienation he feels may find a kindred spirit, viz. Antoine Roquentin, the protagonist in NAUSEA; I would be the 1st to admit that, although it is a novel, a work of fiction with didactic overtones and characters who put in practice Sartre's existentialism, it isn't and I don't believe was, written with the intent of creating a novel qua novel, e.g., Ulysses, Crime &amp; Punishment, The Trial, Remembrance of Things Past or The Dead Souls, which are examples of the novel at its acme. However, if one wishes to learn or understand the singularly breathtaking philosophy of Sartre, NAUSEA would be a great starting point, followed by a reading (or, if a you're lucky enough to be in or near a city where they happen to be staging them) any or all of his plays, e.g., NO EXIT or THE FLIES just to name two; then, after going through his longer novel, The Age of Reason, if you have a burning desire to read the unmasked, existentialist bible, BEING AND NOTHINGNESS, and you are willing to read every sentence, every page, you will be left as an, if not satisfied in terms of a philosophical "epiphany", you will at least come away with a better understanding of the man, his thinking, his philosophy and his sensitive, expressionistic, albeit occasionally a bit abstract. Jean-Paul Sartre was a genius. If only more Americans weren't addicted to mass commercialistic media and the whole cult of celebrity, maybe we'd be better able to stop the worsening of our society by realizing the hypocrisy within in order to be better able to spot it from without. KM. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=french">french</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer325170313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8063655011225916127">‎‭L'etre et le neant, essai d'ontologie phenomenologique‬ = Being and Nothingness, Jean-Paul Sartre<br/>Being and Nothingness: An Essay on Phenomenological Ontology, sometimes subtitled A Phenomenological Essay on Ontology, is a 1943 book by the philosopher Jean-Paul Sartre, in which the author asserts the individual's existence as prior to the individual's essence and seeks to demonstrate that free will exists. While a prisoner of war in 1940 and 1941, Sartre read Martin Heidegger's Being and Time (</span>
  <span id="freeText8063655011225916127" style="display:none">‎‭L'etre et le neant, essai d'ontologie phenomenologique‬ = Being and Nothingness, Jean-Paul Sartre<br />Being and Nothingness: An Essay on Phenomenological Ontology, sometimes subtitled A Phenomenological Essay on Ontology, is a 1943 book by the philosopher Jean-Paul Sartre, in which the author asserts the individual's existence as prior to the individual's essence and seeks to demonstrate that free will exists. While a prisoner of war in 1940 and 1941, Sartre read Martin Heidegger's Being and Time (1927). Heidegger's work, an ontological investigation through the lens and method of Husserlian phenomenology (Edmund Husserl was Heidegger's teacher), initiated Sartre's own philosophical inquiry. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز نخست ماه فوریه سال 2010 میلادی<br />عنوان: هستی و نیستی : پدیده شناسی عالم هستی؛ اثر: ژان پل سارتر؛ مترجم: عنایت الله شکیباپور؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، دنیای کتاب، چاپ نخست ؟؟؟ تجدید چاپ پس از سی و چند سال در 1389، در 432 ص، شابک: 9789643460532؛ <br />عنوان: هستی و نیستی: جستاری در هستی شناسی پدیدار شناختی؛ نویسنده: ژان پل سارتر؛ برگردان: مهستی بحرینی؛ تهران، نیلوفر، 1394؛ در 856 ص؛ شابک: 9789644486456؛ موضوع: اگزیستانسیالیسم - سده 20 م <br />کتاب با ترجمه ابراهیم صدقیانی نیز در تهران، جامی، 1389 در دو جلد منتشر شده است<br /><br />‮هستی و نیستی یک پیش گفتار است و چهار بخش، یک اثر فلسفی تمام عیار از سده بیستم میلادی ... نقش بازی کردن پیشخدمت کافه، و یا «تصادف موتورسیکلت»، زنی که وانمود می‌کند متوجه نیست که مردی دستش را گرفته، همه انگار مثالهایی از همین زندگی روز، رقصی میانه ی میدان، بین هستن و نیستن...؛ در کتاب حاضر، «ژان پل سارتر» به بررسی فلسفه بنیادی، با عنوان «فلسفه هستی و نیستی»، پرداخته است. سارتر در این کتاب، ضمن یک بیان کامل، از اثبات هستی و نیستی، و رابطه ای که میان آنان وجود دارد، به ذکر تمام مقولات عالم هستی پرداخته، و عواطف انسانی نظری همچون: وجدان، عشق، نفرت، هوس و سایر تضادهای درونی، و تعارض نفسانی را، به صورت آشکار و مستدل، شرح داده است، که در فلسفه ی او روح «کانت» و «هگل» توامان مشاهده میشود. «سارتر» برای انسان، دو هستی قائل شده، یکی هستی برای خود، و آن یکی دیگر، هستی برای دیگری. ایشان این هستی دوم را، جزء جدانشدنی هستی برای خود میدانند، و باور دارند که هر انسان زنده، با هستی دیگری که در وجود او فعالیت دارد، زندگی میکند، و رابطه ی میان این دو هستی، یک هستی دیگر، به نام هستی در درون را ارائه میکنند. چرا که ایشان باور دارند عواطف انسانی ذکر شده، به هم پیوسته نیستند، و خوانشگر باید مفهوم هر کدام را به صورت جداگانه، درک کند. همچنین در کتاب آراء «سارتر» درباره ی مکتب «اگزیستانسیالیسم (اصالت انسان)» به اختصار آورده شده است. ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_20489158" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20489158" />
    <a title="Tyler " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1096417-tyler"><img alt="Tyler " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377822301p2/1096417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20489158?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler " href="/user/show/1096417-tyler">Tyler </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780671483883" href="/book/show/2868558-being-and-nothingness">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Every Thinker</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1096417-tyler?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20489158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12196037161544995030">One of the more cold-serious works I've read, this treatise exerts a strange power that forces readers onward despite the dense subject matter and clunky English translation.<br/><br/>The subject is man's experience of reality. Here you have a rigorous scouring of the subject resulting in a proof of human freedom so thorough you'll never fool with hard determinism again. Every aspect of consciousness is traced in all its implications. After reading this there seems little more to be said about the basis i</span>
  <span id="freeText12196037161544995030" style="display:none">One of the more cold-serious works I've read, this treatise exerts a strange power that forces readers onward despite the dense subject matter and clunky English translation.<br /><br />The subject is man's experience of reality. Here you have a rigorous scouring of the subject resulting in a proof of human freedom so thorough you'll never fool with hard determinism again. Every aspect of consciousness is traced in all its implications. After reading this there seems little more to be said about the basis in reality of human thought. The unique effect of reading the book, for me, came from exploring my own mind and thoughts for insight as I followed what Sartre said. <br /><br />The scope of the book treats conscious thought in isolation. You need a fairly good philosophical vocabulary to read it, as well as a highlighter. Even then, some of the points are so abstruse you have to pause and think, often on each paragraph. Joseph Catalano's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30524.A_Commentary_on_Jean_Paul_Sartre_s_Being_and_Nothingness" title="A Commentary on Jean-Paul Sartre's Being and Nothingness by Joseph S. Catalano" rel="nofollow noopener">A Commentary on Jean-Paul Sartre's Being and Nothingness</a> is a valuable companion. Those considering reading this book may want to read Catalano alongside it. <br /> <br />As with many existential works, this study tends to ignore external influences on thought. Sartre does pose the problem of the "situation limit" to human freedom, but without exploring it in any detail. As a result, the outward, natural necessity that provides the context for human freedom receives scant attention. Thence comes the sense of a human consciousness unbounded in its freedom. <br /><br />Sartre's characterization of the human mind possessing "absolute freedom and absolute responsibility" takes on a metaphysical aura; this, as much as anything, accounts for the book's ability to engage one's feelings. The reading of this work is actually more rewarding than what one might learn from it. What an intriguing effect for such an academic work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12196037161544995030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3355864130" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3355864130" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3355864130?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3355864130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13333732341954633951">In my more jejune years, in the seventies and eighties, Jean-Paul Sartre played Pied Piper to my bemused Flower Child of Hamelin!<br/><br/>It was that bad. <br/><br/>You see, I was of two minds on this dangerous book - the first part of it was well spoken - but the second was distinctly dangerous for the ingénu I was. <br/><br/>But it’s mere gibberish, I’m afraid, to most of the rest of us. So harmless.<br/><br/>Why did I have two minds? <br/><br/>Because Sartre had two minds on it himself: on one side, his more cerebral braininess, and the oth</span>
  <span id="freeText13333732341954633951" style="display:none">In my more jejune years, in the seventies and eighties, Jean-Paul Sartre played Pied Piper to my bemused Flower Child of Hamelin!<br /><br />It was that bad. <br /><br />You see, I was of two minds on this dangerous book - the first part of it was well spoken - but the second was distinctly dangerous for the ingénu I was. <br /><br />But it’s mere gibberish, I’m afraid, to most of the rest of us. So harmless.<br /><br />Why did I have two minds? <br /><br />Because Sartre had two minds on it himself: on one side, his more cerebral braininess, and the other, his fallen body-mind - the way of all flesh. <br /><br />And thus fittingly, he divides the book into two main parts, one dealing with the constricted moral freedom of pure being, and the other, with the bondage of amoral facticity.<br /><br />But guess what? Each is mutually exclusive in the real world. Hence for him it was all endless aporia because he sided with the world.<br /><br />The better to gain a wider readership?<br /><br />But as Chogyam Trungoa says in his little bestseller Shambhala, one of our worlds is the hopeful world of sunrise, Shambhala - and the other is the samsaric world of sunset, with all its outré accoutrements and subsequent shame.<br /><br />You pays your money and you takes your chances!<br /><br />Says it all, don’t it? For Shambhala is Augustine’s City of God.<br /><br />So I chose the fresh, sunrise world over Sartre’s smelly and irreducible paradox. And that has its own aporias...<br /><br />You see, the world is still run by Pharisees, as it was in 32 AD in Ancient Palestine. Folks are still mocked for infinitely less than Jesus accomplished. You’ve got to pick up every stitch!<br /><br />Yes, friends, if you go out on life’s limb, make darned sure you’re close enough to the trunk to clamber right back down again.<br /><br />But staying in line is hard when your heart screams foul at the world’s hypocritical judgement of you when it sees you wearing it openly on your sleeve...<br /><br />Martin Luther points out that the Way of Faith is murder when, like Paul to the Galatians, you assert the Primacy of Love over the Law. You attract attacks by the same kinda Trolls as Pope Francis daily faces in the Curia. Wimps need not apply for that position!<br /><br />You know, I had a wise mentor back in those same 1980’s when I read Sartre - old Bob. Bob always use to say, “A man’s got to do what a man’s got to do!”<br /><br />Life stinks, but we’ve got our marching orders! Being’s prerequisites for a Christian are an insurmountable wall in his worst moments.<br /><br />It’s just too bad Jean-Paul Sartre thought he could make the obduracy of Being easier by bucking the system.<br /><br />By all means, go with the flow, friends. Go with the flow...<br /><br />But bear your own Cross with Fortitude.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13333732341954633951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1293641855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1293641855" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1293641855?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=sartre">sartre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=heidegger">heidegger</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=cul-poli-phil-art">cul-poli-phil-art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=lit-krit">lit-krit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1293641855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer813978583366135647"><b>IMMERSE ME IN YOUR SPLENDOUR!</b><br/><br/><i>"This is the one!"</i><br/><br/>[The Stone Roses]<br/><br/>It helps to have read Heidegger's <i>"Being and Time"</i> before this volume that some describe as a companion, others as a critique (it's both, actually).<br/><br/>Heidegger writes like someone who is a reader; Sartre like someone who is both a reader and a writer. This is not to deny that Heidegger is a good writer. Just that Sartre is a better one. <br/><br/>Sartre wrote while Heidegger's ideas were still fresh. He agreed with many, disagreed with some, fi</span>
  <span id="freeText813978583366135647" style="display:none"><b>IMMERSE ME IN YOUR SPLENDOUR!</b><br><br><i>"This is the one!"</i><br><br>[The Stone Roses]<br><br>It helps to have read Heidegger's <i>"Being and Time"</i> before this volume that some describe as a companion, others as a critique (it's both, actually).<br><br>Heidegger writes like someone who is a reader; Sartre like someone who is both a reader and a writer. This is not to deny that Heidegger is a good writer. Just that Sartre is a better one. <br><br>Sartre wrote while Heidegger's ideas were still fresh. He agreed with many, disagreed with some, fine-tuned others, and finished the project that Heidegger set himself, but failed to complete. Naturally, Sartre accomplished something that was different from what Heidegger had intended at any stage of his career. Two philosophers, at least two opinions.<br><br>Sartre described his work as <i>"an essay on phenomenological ontology,"</i> its goal to set down <i>"the basis for a general theory of being."</i><br><br>It is a systematic, analytical work. It has the hallmarks of the type of system that Heidegger envisaged but failed to achieve, because he segmented his project, stopped at the first phase (which was enough to gain him a professorial post), started to question and doubt subsequently, revised, and went on to other interests (including the reconciliation of his philosophy with National Socialism).<br><br>Ontology is an extremely speculative, subjective, arbitrary and even metaphorical study.<br><br>Sartre doesn't accord Heidegger any particular privileged status. He is simply one more philosopher trying to address issues posed by philosophy in general and Husserl in particular. Both are trying to feel their way in the dark, recording their perspectives and impressions as they progress. <br><br>You might not agree with everything that Sartre (or Heidegger, for that matter) wrote. At least, unlike <i>"Being and Time",</i> you can tell from the text of <i>"Being and Nothingness"</i> itself, what ideas and arguments belong to Sartre, what he has adopted from his predecessors (who are acknowledged), and what his differences and disagreements are. This is an argumentative work which tries to tease out the truth, rather than one that simply proclaims its truth imperiously and ex cathedra.<br><br>Ultimately, I found Sartre's work to be a more honest and accountable study than <i>"Being and Time".</i><br><br>Notwithstanding its length, it is also a more engaging literary experience for a reader, once (if at all) you become comfortable with the terminology of phenomenology and ontology.<br><br><i>"Being and Nothingness"</i> works hard to be both a philosophical and a literary experience. As a result, it is a source of greater illumination.<br><br><br><br><b>THE INSISTENCE OF THE CARTESIAN SUBJECT<br>[A Subjective Précis]:</b><br><br>Consciousness is what negates, differentiates, separates, determines, designates. It differentiates the Subject from the Object, and the Self from the Other. In order to identify itself, consciousness in the form of Being-for-itself turns inward and negates the Being-in-itself. Yet, Being-for-itself is nothing other than Being-in-itself. It is one and the same thing. Being is separated by nothingness. Consciousness identifies and chooses possibilities for being. Freedom is action in pursuit of possibilities. Freedom is the burden or responsibility of making our own choices. Freedom is the recognition and embrace of the possibilities of our own being. Bad faith occurs when consciousness eschews its responsibility to itself.<br><br><br><br><b>AT 38:</b><br><br>Heidegger and Sartre were both 38 at the time of publication of their respective works, <i>"Being and Time"</i> and <i>"Being and Nothingness".</i><br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1446018473i/16736015._SX540_.png" width="400" height="280" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Meredith Joy Ostrom (Miriam) in "The Ninth Cloud"</i><br><br><br><b>THE MERE POSSIBILITY OF A RENASCENCE:</b><br><br><b>The Extreme Radicalisation of a Potentiality</b><br><br><i>"...Sartre's convictions are really closer to Heidegger's than to anyone else's. Indeed, the least inadequate capsule classification is to make of him the extreme radicalisation of a potentiality inherent in Heidegger's 'Sein und Zeit'.<br><br>"The passion with which he has expressed his convictions has given his philosophy a hard-hitting tone and has tended to spill over into the most exciting literature written by any philosopher since 'Zarathustra'."</i><br><br>Thomas Langan<br> <br><br><b>The Post-structuralist Project</b><br><br><i>"It has been an unspoken goal of the post-structuralist project to render Sartre history -- and thereby to free itself from the weight of his thinking. Yet, to leave Sartre unspeakable through silence is silently to call attention to him as somehow fundamental; it is to suggest his having been given a reading, and call for a rereading."</i><br><br>Steve Martinot<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.ocf.berkeley.edu/~marto/hs-sh.htm">https://www.ocf.berkeley.edu/~marto/h...</a><br><br><br><b>Making An Instrument for His Own Project</b><br><br><i>"In sharp contrast to Heidegger, then, Sartre has no interest in conferring a meaning on (or otherwise deifying) being at the expense of the meaning conferring subject, for he believes (and not without good reason) that the individual’s conscious experience of the world is at the heart of the phenomenological impulse. <br><br>"Accordingly, Heideggerians such as Hubert Dreyfus, who believe that the “theory of consciousness” offered in Being and Nothingness is only a “misguided reformulation of Being and Time,” miss the point. <br><br>"Sartre does not aim to reformulate Being and Time any more than Heidegger aimed to reformulate Husserl’s phenomenology. <br><br>"Like all philosophers, he only aims to take from his predecessors what is useful for his own project, which, in Sartre’s case, revolves around the phenomenological freedom of the subject."</i><br><br>David Sherman<br><br><br><b>Understood for the First Time</b><br><br><i>"In 1946 in 'Letter on Humanism' Heidegger presented what is sometimes thought of as a devastating critique of Sartre, but only a year earlier in a note to himself he endorsed Sartre's reading of 'Being and Time'.<br><br>"Heidegger wrote in relation to Corbin's translation of 'What is Metaphysics?': 'Decisive effect on Sartre: from there 'Being and Time' understood for the first time.'<br><br>"Indeed on October 28, 1945 Heidegger wrote to Sartre, not only acknowledging that in 'Being and Nothingness' Sartre had shown a level of understanding of 'Being and Time' that he had not found elsewhere, but also recognising him as an independent thinker in his own right. <br><br>"Acting completely out of character, Heidegger told Sartre that he accepted Sartre's critique of the account of 'Mitsein' in 'Being and Time' and he acknowledged the legitimacy of Sartre's insistence on being-for-others...<br><br>"It was a remarkably conciliatory letter, even if one cannot avoid the suspicion that Heidegger was soliciting Sartre's help during what after Germany's defeat was a difficult time for him."</i><br><br>Robert Bernasconi<br><br><br><br><b>A SEA OF POSSIBILITIES: A SUBJECTIVE [DI-] VERSIFICATION OF VARIOUS SARTRESIAN MEDITATIONS<br>[Mostly in the Words of Sartre]:</b><br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><br><u><b>I-TELEMACHUS</b></u><br><br><b>The Possibility of Self-Denial</b> <br><br>What are we to say is <br>The being of man who has <br>The possibility of <br>Denying himself?<br><br><b>The Hole</b><br><br>The For-itself is nothing<br>But the pure nihilation<br>Of the In-itself;<br>It is like a hole of being<br>At the heart of Being.<br>The For-itself is<br>The nothingness<br>Of the In-itself.<br><br><b>The Existing Moon</b> <br><br>What the crescent moon lacks,<br>In order to be a full moon,<br>Is a fragment of moon.<br><br><b>The Crack</b> <br><br>Nothingness <br>Is a crack <br>In the egg <br>Of being.<br><br><b>The End</b><br><br>The end and the goal<br>Of the nihilation<br>Which I am<br>Is the in-itself.<br><br><b>The Vanishing</b> <br><br>Without this <br>In-itself<br>Which I deny,<br>I should vanish<br>Into nothingness.<br><br><u><b>II-NESTOR</b></u> <br><br><b>Consciousness of Being</b> <br><br>Nothing is in<br>Consciousness<br>Which is not<br>Consciousness<br>Of being.<br><br><b>The Nihilation of the Past Being</b> <br><br>Consciousness continually <br>Experiences itself <br>As the nihilation of <br>Its past being.<br><br><b>The Nihilation of the Existent</b> <br><br>Aspiration is<br>The denial<br>Of the existent<br>In favour of<br>The possible.<br><br><b>Separated by Nothingness</b> <br><br>Human reality carries <br>Nothingness within itself <br>As the nothing which <br>Separates its present <br>From all its past.<br><br><b>Nothingness Between</b><br><br>Between the nihilated<br>In-itself and <br>The projected in-itself,<br>The for-itself<br>Is nothingness.<br><br><b>No Escape</b> <br><br>The for-itself<br>Cannot escape<br>The in-itself,<br>Because the for-itself<br>Is nothing,<br>And it is<br>Separated<br>From the in-itself<br>By nothing.<br><br><b>The Cleavage</b> <br><br>Nothing is like <br>The nothingness <br>That is <br>The cleavage <br>Between <br>Two breasts.<br><br><b>Keeping Abreast of Time [or Two]</b> <br><br>The past and the present <br>Are separated <br>By nothingness <br>And as unalike <br>As two breasts.<br><br><u><b>III-PROTEUS</b></u> <br><br><b>Being, Possibility and Time</b> <br><br>All being strives to<br>Become possibility<br>In the course of time.<br><br><b>The Future</b> <br><br>The Future is not,<br>It is possibilised.<br>The Future is<br>The continual<br>Possibilisation<br>Of possibilities.<br><br><b>Future Perfect Possibility</b> <br><br>To be its own possibility,<br>To be defined by it,<br>Is to be defined by <br>That part of itself<br>Which it is not,<br>To be defined by <br>An escape from itself<br>Towards a future possibility.<br><br><b>The Projected Future</b> <br><br>What I project <br>As my future being <br>Is always nihilated <br>And reduced <br>To the rank <br>Of possibility, <br>Because the future <br>Which I am remains <br>Out of my reach.<br><br><b>A Meaning Out of Reach</b> <br><br>From the moment I define myself<br>Insofar as I am what I am not,<br>And am not what I am,<br>I am thrown towards a meaning<br>That is out of reach.<br><br><u><b>IV-CALYPSO</b></u> <br><br><b>In the Mode</b> <br><br>I am the self <br>Which I will be, <br>In the mode of <br>Not being it.<br><br><b>A Certain Coincidence</b> <br><br>Each for-itself is haunted<br>By the presence of that<br>With which it should coincide<br>In order to be itself.<br><br><b>A Tale of Two Halfs</b> <br><br>Possibility is <br>The glass half full,<br>While nothingness is<br>The glass half empty.<br><br><b>Something Possible</b> <br><br>The possible is the something<br>Which the for-itself lacks<br>In order to be itself. <br><br><b>For Heidegger...</b> <br><br>To be is to be<br>My own possibilities.<br>It is a mode of being<br>In which I make myself be.<br><br><b>Being and Possibility</b> <br><br>Each being-for-itself <br>Strives towards the possibility <br>Of being what it is not,<br>Which (contra Sartre?)<br>Is not nothingness,<br>But possibility.<br><br><u><b>V-LOTUS EATERS</b></u><br><br><b>Consciousness of the World</b> <br><br>What I seek<br>In the face of the world<br>Is the coincidence<br>With a for-itself<br>Which I am and which is<br>Consciousness of the world.<br><br><b>The Horizon of the World</b> <br><br>It is in time<br>That the for-itself is<br>Its own possibilities<br>In the mode of not being.<br>It is in time<br>That my possibilities appear<br>On the horizon of the world<br>Which they make mine.<br><br><b>The Present</b> <br><br>Present is<br>Opposed to<br>Absent,<br>As well as<br>To past.<br><br><u><b>VI-HADES</b></u><br><br><b>(Condemned) To Be Free</b> <br><br>The For-Itself<br>Is free.<br>Its freedom<br>Is to itself<br>Its own limit.<br>To be free<br>Is to be <br>Condemned<br>To be free.<br>I am condemned <br>To be wholly <br>Responsible<br>For myself.<br><br><b>Bad Faith</b> <br><br>Consciousness <br>Constitutes itself<br>In its own flesh<br>As the nihilation<br>Of a possibility<br>Which another <br>Human reality projects<br>As its possibility.<br>Instead of directing<br>Its negation outward,<br>It turns it toward itself.<br><br><b>Flight and Pursuit</b> <br><br>The for-itself<br>Attempts to escape<br>Its factual <br>Existence.<br>This flight <br>Takes place toward<br>An impossible future.<br>Thus the for-itself<br>Is both a flight <br>And a pursuit.<br><br><u><b>VII-AEOLUS</b></u><br><br><b>The Object</b> <br><br>The object is<br>That which is not<br>My consciousness.<br><br><b>Objectivity</b> <br><br>I make the Other<br>Lose himself<br>In the world<br>Which is mine<br>By the sole fact that<br>He is for me the one<br>Who I have to not-be.<br>I make the Other be<br>In the midst of the world.<br><br><b>The Mediator</b> <br><br>The Other is<br>The indispensable <br>Mediator between <br>Myself and me.<br><br><b>Hegelian Gameplay</b> <br><br>The road of<br>Interiority<br>Passes through<br>The Other.<br>As I appear<br>To the Other,<br>So I am.<br>Since the Other<br>Is such as<br>He appears<br>To me,<br>And my being<br>Depends upon<br>The Other,<br>The way in which<br>I appear<br>Depends on <br>The way<br>In which<br>The Other<br>Appears<br>To me.<br><br><b>The Other's Look</b> <br><br>To be looked at<br>Is to apprehend oneself<br>As the unknown object<br>Of unknowable appraisals.<br><br><b>Shame</b> <br><br>I am ashamed<br>Of myself<br>Before the Other.<br><br><b>Vanity</b> <br><br>Vanity impels me<br>To get hold <br>Of the Other,<br>So that I may<br>Discover there<br>My own object-state,<br>So that he may <br>Release to me<br>The secret of my being.<br><br><u><b>VIII-LESTRYGONIANS</b></u> <br><br><b>The Fall</b> <br><br>I have fallen <br>Into the world<br>In the midst<br>Of things.<br>Through the world<br>I make known<br>To myself<br>What I am.<br><br><b>Being in the Midst of the World</b> <br><br>The ultimate project<br>Of Being-for-itself<br>Is being-in-the-midst-of-the-world.<br><br><b>The Absolute Flow</b> <br><br>My senses are<br>In the midst<br>Of a world;<br>They are in and through<br>The absolute flow<br>Of my world<br>Toward the Other.<br><br><u><b>IX-SCYLLA AND CHARYBDIS</b></u><br><br><b>Engagement</b> <br><br>I live my selfness<br>In its concrete projection<br>Toward this or that<br>Particular end.<br>I exist only<br>As engaged.<br><br><b>Always Engaged</b> <br><br>The freedom<br>Of the for-itself<br>Is always<br>Engaged.<br><br><u><b>X-WANDERING ROCKS</b></u><br><br><b>Relation</b> <br><br>The for-itself is<br>The foundation<br>Of all negativity<br>And of all relation.<br>The for-itself is <br>Relation.<br><br><b>Conflict</b> <br><br>The essence <br>Of the relations<br>Between consciousnesses<br>Is not the Mitsein;<br>It is conflict.<br><br><b>Death</b> <br><br>To die is <br>To lose all<br>Possibility<br>Of revealing <br>Oneself as<br>Subject to an Other.<br><br><u><b>XI-SIRENS</b></u><br><br><b>The Flesh</b> <br><br>The flesh is the<br>Pure contingency<br>Of presence.<br><br><b>The Body</b> <br><br>The body is the<br>Contingent form<br>Which is taken up<br>By the necessity<br>Of my contingent form.<br><br><b>The Other's Body</b> <br><br>The Other's body<br>Is the pure fact<br>Of the Other's presence<br>In my world<br>As a being-there<br>Which is expressed<br>As a being-as-this.<br><br><b>A Body Known</b> <br><br>I exist<br>For myself<br>As a body<br>Known by<br>The Other.<br>I feel myself<br>Touched by<br>The Other<br>In my factual <br>Existence.<br>It is my <br>Being-there-for-others,<br>For which I am <br>Responsible.<br><br><b>Making Itself Body</b> <br><br>The being which desires<br>Is consciousness<br>Making itself body.<br><br><b>Seduction</b> <br><br>By seduction I aim <br>At constituting myself <br>As a fullness of being, <br>A meaningful object, <br>And at making myself<br>Recognised as such.<br><br><u><b>XII-CYCLOPS</b></u><br><br><b>The Captive</b> <br><br>Each of the lovers<br>Is the captive<br>Of the Other.<br><br><b>Alienated Freedom</b> <br><br>The one who wants<br>To be loved,<br>By the mere fact<br>Of wanting someone<br>To love him,<br>Alienates his freedom.<br><br><b>Complicity</b> <br><br>I am the <br>Accomplice<br>Of my own<br>Desire,<br>Which has fallen<br>Wholly into <br>Complicity<br>With the body. <br><br><b>Only Body</b> <br><br>Desire is not <br>Only the desire<br>Of the Other's body;<br>It is the lived project<br>Of being swallowed up<br>In the body.<br>Consciousness allows itself<br>To go over to the body,<br>Wishes to be the body,<br>And to be only body.<br><br><b>Ensnarement</b> <br><br>Desire is <br>The ensnarement<br>Of a body<br>By the world.<br>Consciousness<br>Is ensnared<br>In a body<br>Which is engulfed<br>In the world.<br><br><b>Incarnation</b> <br><br>In desire,<br>I incarnate<br>My consciousness,<br>I incarnate myself,<br>In order to realise<br>The incarnation <br>Of the Other.<br><br><b>The Caress</b> <br><br>The caress is<br>The ensemble<br>Of those rituals<br>Which incarnate<br>The Other.<br><br><b>Double Reciprocal Incarnation</b> <br><br>The caress causes<br>The Other's body<br>To be born,<br>Through pleasure,<br>For the Other -<br>And for myself -<br>In such a way<br>That my body<br>Is made flesh<br>In order to touch<br>The Other's body,<br>That is, by<br>Caressing itself with<br>The Other's body,<br>Rather than by <br>Caressing her/him.<br><br><u><b>XIII-NAUSICAA</b></u><br><br><b>The Mode of Not Being</b> <br><br>Anguish is my consciousness <br>Of being my own future, <br>In the mode of not-being.<br><br><b>Anguish I</b><br><br>The for-itself<br>Apprehends<br>Itself in anguish<br>As a being<br>Compelled to decide<br>The meaning of being<br>Within it and<br>Everywhere <br>Outside of it.<br>The one who realises <br>In anguish his condition<br>As being thrown<br>Into a responsibility<br>Has no longer either<br>Remorse or regret or excuse.<br>He is no longer <br>Anything but a freedom.<br>Most of the time<br>We flee anguish<br>In bad faith.<br><br><b>Anguish II</b> <br><br>Anguish<br>Manifests<br>Our freedom<br>To our <br>Consciousness.<br>Anguish is a consequence<br>Of taking your Self<br>Seriously.<br><br><b>Nausea</b> <br><br>My knowledge<br>Extends my nausea<br>Toward that which<br>It is for others.<br>For it is<br>The Other<br>Who grasps<br>My nausea,<br>Precisely as flesh<br>And with the<br>Nauseous character<br>Of all flesh.<br><br><u><b>XIV-OXEN OF THE SUN</b></u><br><br><b>Work as a Mode of Alienation</b> <br><br>The worker - servile or not -<br>Experiences in work his<br>Being-an-instrument for others.<br>Work, when not strictly destined<br>For the ends of the worker himself,<br>Is a mode of alienation.<br><br><b>Revolution</b> <br><br>The education<br>And reflection<br>Necessary<br>To conceive<br>Of a social state<br>In which suffering<br>Would not exist.<br><br><u><b>XV-CIRCE</b></u><br><br><b>Action</b> <br><br>To act is to<br>Modify the shape<br>Of the world.<br><br><b>The Act</b> <br><br>To be is to act,<br>And to cease to act<br>Is to cease to be.<br>The act is<br>The expression<br>Of freedom.<br>I am an existent<br>Who learns<br>His freedom<br>Through his acts.<br><br><b>The Upsurge</b> <br><br>The present<br>Is the upsurge<br>Of the act.<br><br><b>Freedom I</b> <br><br>For the for-itself,<br>To be is to<br>Nihilate<br>The in-itself<br>Which it is.<br>Freedom is<br>Nothing other<br>Than this nihilation.<br><br><b>Freedom's Escape</b> <br><br>Freedom is <br>Not free<br>Not to exist<br>Or not to be free.<br>Freedom<br>Cannot escape <br>Its existence.<br><br><u><b>XVI-EUMAEUS</b></u><br><br><b>The Choice of an End</b> <br><br>Freedom is the choice<br>Of an end<br>In terms of the past.<br>Conversely,<br>The past is what it is,<br>Only in relation to <br>The end chosen. <br><br><b>The Illumination of the End</b> <br><br>The end<br>Illuminates<br>What is.<br>What-is<br>Takes on its meaning<br>Only when it is surpassed<br>Toward the future.<br><br><b>The Choice</b> <br><br>We are fully<br>Conscious<br>Of the choice<br>Which we are.<br><br><u><b>XVII-ITHACA</b></u><br><br><b>Free-To-Change</b> <br><br>Freedom implies<br>The existence<br>Of an environment<br>To be changed.<br>To be free is<br>To be free-to-change.<br><br><b>Free-To-Do</b> <br><br>To be free<br>Is to be free-to-do,<br>To be free-in-the-world.<br><br><b>Freedom II</b><br><br>The for-itself<br>By its upsurge<br>Causes the in-itself<br>To come into the world.<br>It is our freedom<br>Which is responsible<br>For the fact that<br>There are things<br>And that we are<br>Separated from them.<br><br><u><b>XVIII-PENELOPE</b></u><br><br><b>Desire I</b><br><br>Desire is a <br>Lack of being.<br><br><b>Desire II</b><br><br>Human reality<br>Is the desire<br>Of being-in-itself.<br><br><b>Desire to Be</b><br><br>It is not enough<br>That I be;<br>I must desire<br>To be.<br><br><b>My Thought</b><br><br>I desire <br>To apprehend<br>My thought<br>As a thing, <br>And the thing<br>As my thought.<br><br><b>Possession</b><br><br>To-be-in-the-world<br>Is to form the project<br>Of possessing<br>The world. <br><br><b>What I Have</b><br><br>I am<br>What<br>I have.<br><br><b>The Known</b><br><br>To know<br>Is to have.<br>The known<br>Is transformed<br>Into me.<br><br><b>The Lover’s Dream</b><br><br>The lover's dream<br>Is to identify<br>The beloved object<br>With himself<br>And still preserve<br>Its individuality;<br>Let the Other<br>Become me<br>Without ceasing<br>To be the Other.<br><br><b>To Be God</b><br><br>Man is the being<br>Whose project<br>Is to be God.<br>To be man<br>Means to reach<br>Toward being God.<br>Man is the desire <br>To be God.<br>Man makes himself man<br>In order to be God.<br><br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/648446660" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/648446660?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671867805" href="/book/show/511340.Being_and_Nothingness">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=footnotes-to-plato">footnotes-to-plato</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=francophilia">francophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=this-and-that">this-and-that</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=supermassive">supermassive</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer648446660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8867068094409017301">Here is this review in podcast form:<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://podcasts.apple.com/us/podcast/review-being-and-nothingness/id1469809686?i=1000442467054'>https://podcasts.apple.com/us/podcast...</a><br/>________________________________<br/><blockquote>
  <i>Slime is the agony of water.</i>
</blockquote><br/>I first heard of this book from my dad. “I had to read this in college,” he told me. “We looked at every type of being. Being-in-myself, being-for-myself, being-of-myself, being-across-myself, being-by-myself. I went crazy trying to read that thing.” Ever since that memorable description, this book has held a special allure for me. It has everything to attra</span>
  <span id="freeText8867068094409017301" style="display:none">Here is this review in podcast form:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://podcasts.apple.com/us/podcast/review-being-and-nothingness/id1469809686?i=1000442467054">https://podcasts.apple.com/us/podcast...</a><br />________________________________<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Slime is the agony of water.</i>
</blockquote><br />I first heard of this book from my dad. “I had to read this in college,” he told me. “We looked at every type of being. Being-in-myself, being-for-myself, being-of-myself, being-across-myself, being-by-myself. I went crazy trying to read that thing.” Ever since that memorable description, this book has held a special allure for me. It has everything to attract a self-styled intellectual: a reputation for difficulty, a hefty bulk, a pompous title, and the imprimatur of a famous name. Clearly I had to read it.<br /><br />Jean-Paul Sartre was the defining intellectual of his time, at least on the European continent. He did everything: writing novels and plays, founding and editing a journal, engaging in political activism, and pioneering a philosophical school: existentialism. This book is the defining monument of that school. An eight-hundred-page treatise on ontology which, somehow, became widely read—or at least widely talked about. Nearly eighty years later, we are still talking about this book. In 2016 Sarah Bakewell released a best-selling book about Sartre’s movement; and a new translation of <i>Being and Nothingness</i> will be released next year. Interest in existentialism has not abated.<br /><br />Yet what is existentialism? And how has it weathered the passing years? This is what I set out to determine, and this review will show whether my attempt bore fruit.<br /><br />One should begin by examining the subtitle of this book: “A Phenomenological Essay on Ontology.” Already we have a contradiction. Phenomenology is a philosophical school founded by Edmund Husserl, which attempted to direct philosophers’ attention back “to the things themselves”—that is, to their own experience of the world. One of Husserl’s most insistent commandments was that the philosopher should “bracket,” or set aside, the old Cartesian question of the reality of these experiences (is the world truly as I perceive it?); rather, the philosopher should simply examine the qualities of the experience itself. Thus, Sartre’s promise of a phenomenological ontology (ontology being the investigation of the fundamental nature of reality) is a flagrant violation of Husserl’s principles.<br /><br />Still, it does have a lot to tell us about Sartre’s method. This book is an attempt to deduce the fundamental categories of being from everyday experience. And this attempt leads Sartre to the two most basic categories of all: being and nothingness. Being is all around us; it is manifest in every object we experience. Sartre defines existing objects as those which are self-identical—that is, objects which simply are what they are—and he dubs this type of being the “in-itself.” Humans, by contrast, cannot be so defined; they are constantly shifting, projecting themselves into an uncertain future. Rather than simply existing, they observe their own existence. Sartre calls this type of human existence the “for-itself.”<br /><br />Already we see the old Cartesian dualism reappearing in these categories. Are we not confronted, once again, with the paradoxes of matter and mind? Not exactly. For Sartre does not consider the in-itself and the for-itself to be two different types of substances. In fact, the for-itself has no existence at all: it is a nothingness. To use Sartre’s expressions, human consciousness can be compared to “little pools of non-being that we encounter in the heart of being,” or elsewhere he says that the for-itself “is like a hole in being at the heart of Being.” The for-itself (a consciousness) is a particular privation of a specific in-itself (a human body), which functions as a nihilation that makes the world appear: for there would not be a “world” as we know it without perception, and perception is, for Sartre, a type of nihilation.<br /><br />Putting aside all of the difficulties with this view, we can examine the consequences which Sartre draws from these two sorts of being. If the for-itself is a nothingness, then it is forever removed from the world around it. That is, it cannot be determined, either by its past or by its environment. In short, it is free—inescapably free. Human behavior can thus never be adequately explained or even excused, since all explanations or excuses presuppose that humans are not fundamentally self-determining. But of course we explain and excuse all the time. We point to economic class, occupation, culture, gender, race, sexuality, upbringing, genetic background, mood—to a thousand different factors in order to understand why people act the way they do.<br /><br />This attempt to treat humans as things rather than free beings Sartre calls “bad faith.” This constitutes the fundamental sin of existentialism. He gives the example of a waiter who so embraces his role as a waiter that his motions become calculated and mechanical; the waiter tries to embody himself in his role to the extent that he gives up his individual freedom and becomes a kind of automaton whose every movement is predictable. But of course life is full of examples of bad faith. I excuse my mistake by saying I hadn’t had my coffee yet; my friend cheats on his girlfriend, but it was because his father cheated on his mother; and so on. <br /><br />This is the basic situation of the for-itself. Yet there is another type of being which Sartre later introduces us to: the for-others. Sartre introduces this category with a characteristically vivid example: Imagine a peeping Tom is looking through a keyhole into a room. His attention is completely fixed on what he sees. Then, suddenly, he hears footsteps coming down the hall; and he immediately becomes aware of himself as a body, as a thing. Sartre considers experiences like this to prove that we cannot doubt the existence of others, since being perceived by others totally changes how we experience ourselves.<br /><br />This allows Sartre to launch into an analysis of human interaction, and particularly into love and sexuality. This analysis bears the obvious influence of Hegel’s famous Master-Slave dialectic, and it centers on the same sorts of paradoxes: the contradictory urges to subjugate and be subjugated, to be embodied and desired, to be free and to be freely chosen, and so on. However, Sartre’s best writing in this vein is not to be found here, but in his great play <i>No Exit</i>, where each character exhibits a particular type of bad faith. All three of the characters wish to be looked at in a particular way, yet each of them is stuck with others whose own particular sort of bad faith renders them unable to look in the “right” way. <br /><br />Sartre concludes from all this that our most fervent desire, and the reason we so often slip into bad faith, is that we wish to be an impossible combination of the in-itself and the for-itself. We want to be the foundation of our own being, a perfect self-identical creature, and yet absolutely free. We want to become gods. But, for Sartre, this is self-contradictory: the in-itself and the for-itself can never coexist. Thus, the idea of God arises as a sort of wish-fulfillment; but God is impossible by definition. As a result, human life “is a useless passion”—a relentless striving to be something which cannot exist.<br /><br />All this may be clearer if we avoid Sartre’s terminology and, instead, compare his philosophy to that of Buddhism (at least, the type of Western Buddhism I’m acquainted with). The mind is constantly searching for a sense of permanent identity. Though the mind is, by nature, groundless, we are uncomfortable with this; we want put ground under our feet. So we seek to identify ourselves with our jobs, our families, our marriages, our hobbies, our success, our money—with any external good that lets us forget that our consciousness is constantly shifting and flowing, and that our identities can never be absolutely determined. So far, Buddhism and Sartrean existentialism have similar diagnoses of our problems. But Buddhism prescribes detachment, while Sartre prescribes the embrace of absolute freedom and the adoption of complete responsibility of our actions.<br /><br />No summary of the book would be complete without Sartre’s critique of Freud. Sartre was clearly intrigued by Freud’s theories and wanted to use them in some way. However, Freud’s unconscious motivations and superconscious censorship is clearly incompatible with Sartre’s philosophy of freedom. In particular, Sartre found it self-contradictory to say that there could be a part of the mind which “wants” without us knowing it, or a part that is able to hide information from our awareness. For Sartre, all consciousness is self-consciousness, and it therefore does not make sense to “want” or “know” something unconsciously. <br /><br />In place of Freud’s psychoanalysis, then, Sartre proposes an existential psychoanalysis. For Sartre, every person is defined by a sort of fundamental choice that determines their stance towards the world (though, strangely, it seems that most people are not aware of having made this choice). It is the task of the existential psychoanalyst is to uncover this fundamental choice by a close examination of everyday actions. Indeed, Sartre believes that everything from one’s preference for onions to one’s aversion to cold water is a consequence of this fundamental choice. Sartre even goes so far as to insist that some things, by virtue of being so clearly suggestive of metaphor, have a universal meaning for the for-itself. As an example of this, he gives “slime”—viscous liquid which Sartre thinks inspires a universal horror of the weight of existence.<br /><br />This fairly well rounds out a summary of the book. So what are we to make of this?<br /><br />The comparison with Heidegger is unavoidable. Sartre himself seems to have encouraged the comparison by giving his metaphysical tome a title redolent of the German professor’s magnum opus. The influence is clear: Sartre wrote <i>Being and Nothingness</i> after reading <i>Being and Time</i> during his brief imprisonment in a prisoner-of-war camp; and Heidegger is referenced throughout the book. Nevertheless, I think it would be inaccurate to describe Sartre as a follower of Heidegger, or his philosophy merely as an interpretation of Heidegger’s. Indeed, I think that the superficial similarities between the two thinkers (stylistic obscurity, disregard of religion and ethics, a focus on human experience, a concern with “being”) mask far more important differences.<br /><br />Heidegger’s project, insofar as I understand it, is radically anti-Cartesian. He sought to replace the thinking and observing ego with the Dasein, a being thrown into the world, a being fundamentally ensconced in a community and surrounded by tools ready-to-hand. For Heidegger, the Cartesian perspective—of withdrawing from the world and deliberately reflecting and reasoning—is derivative of, and inferior to, this far more fundamental relationship to being. Sartre could not be further from this. Sartre’s perspective, to the contrary, is insistently Cartesian and subjectivist; it is the philosophy of a single mind urgently investigating its experience. Further, the concept of “freedom” plays almost no role in Heidegger’s philosophy; indeed, I believe he would criticize the very idea of free choice as enmeshed in the Cartesian framework he hoped to destroy.<br /><br />In method, then, Sartre is far closer to Husserl—another professed Cartesian—than to Heidegger. However, as we observed above, Sartre breaks Husserl’s most fundamental tenet by using subjective experiences to investigate being; and this was done clearly under the influence of Heidegger. These two, along with Freud, and Hegel, constitute the major intellectual influences on Sartre.<br /><br />It should be no surprise, then, that Sartre’s style often verges on the obscure. Many passages in this book are comparable in ugliness and density to those German masters of opacity (Freud excluded). Heidegger is the most obvious influence here: for Sartre, like Heidegger, enjoys using clunky hyphenated terms and repurposing quotidian words in order to give them a special meaning. There is an important difference, however. When I did decipher Sartre’s more difficult passages, I usually found that the inky murkiness was rather unnecessary.<br /><br />Believe me when I say that I am no lover of Heidegger’s writing. Nevertheless, I think Heidegger’s tortured locutions are more justifiable than Sartre’s, for Heidegger was attempting to express something that is truly counter-intuitive, at least in the Western philosophical tradition; whereas Sartre’s philosophy, whatever novelties it possesses, is far more clearly in the mainline of Cartesian thinking. As a result, Sartre’s adventures in jargon come across as mere displays of pomp—a bejewelled robe he dons in order to appear more weighty—and, occasionally, as mere abuses of language, concealing simple points in false paradoxes.<br /><br />This is a shame, for when Sartre wished he could be quite a powerful writer. And, indeed, the best sections of this book are when Sartre switches from his psuedo-Heideggerian tone to that of the French novelist. The most memorable passages in this book are Sartre’s illustrations of his theories: the aforementioned waiter, or the Peeping Tom, or the passage on skiing. Whatever merit Sartre had as a philosopher, he was undoubtedly a genius in capturing the intricacies of subjective experience—the turns of thought and twinges of emotion that rush through the mind in everyday situations. <br /><br />But what are we to make of his system? To my mind, the most immediately objectionable aspect is his idea of nothingness. Nothing is just that—nothing: a complete lack of qualities, attributes, or activity of any kind. Indeed, if a nothingness can be defined at all, it must be via elimination: by excluding every existing thing. It seems incoherent, then, to say that the human mind is a nothingness, and is therefore condemned to be free. Consciousness has many definite qualities and, besides that, is constantly active and (in Sartre’s opinion at least) able to choose itself and change the world. How can a nothingness do that? And this is putting to the side the striking question of how the human brain can produce a complete absence of being. Maybe I am taking Sartre’s point too literally; but it is fair to say that he provides no account of how this nothingness came into being.<br /><br />Once this idea of nothingness is called into question, the rest of Sartre’s conclusions are on extremely shaky ground. Sartre’s idea of freedom is especially suspect. If human consciousness is not separated from the world and from its past by a nothingness, then Sartre’s grand pronouncements of total freedom and total responsibility become dubious. To me it seems unlikely to the highest degree that, of all the known objects in the universe, including all of the animals (some of which are closely related to us), humans are the only things that are exempt from the chain of causality that binds everything together.<br /><br />Besides finding it implausible, I also cannot help finding Sartre’s idea of total freedom and responsibility to be morally dubious. He himself, so far as I know, never managed to make his system compatible with a system of ethics. In any case, an emphasis on total responsibility can easily lead to a punitive mentality. According to Sartre, everyone deserves their fate.<br /><br />Admittedly I do think his conception of “bad faith” is useful. Whether or not we are metaphysically “free,” we often have more power over a situation than we admit. Denying our responsibility can lead to inauthenticity and immorality. And Sartre’s embrace of freedom can be a healthy antidote to an apathetic despair. Still, I do not think an elaborate ontological system is necessary in order to make this point.<br /><br />Reading Sartre nowadays, I admit that it is difficult to take his conclusions seriously. For one, the next generation of French intellectuals set to work demonstrating that our freedom is constrained by society (Bourdeiu), psychology (Lacan), language (Derrida), and history (Foucault), among other factors. (Of course, these intellectual projects were not necessarily any more solid than Sartre’s.) More importantly, Sartre’s system seems to be so completely bound up in both his times and his own psychology—two things which he denied could determine human behavior—that it ironically belies his conclusions. (As an example of the latter influence, Sartre’s revulsion and even horror of sex is apparent throughout the book, especially in the strange section on “slime.”) <br /><br />In the end I was somewhat disappointed by this work. And I think my disappointment is ultimately a consequence of Sartre’s method: phenomenological ontology. It is simply incorrect to believe that we can closely interrogate our own experiences to determine the fundamental categories of being. Admittedly, Sartre is not entirely averse to making logical argument; but too many of his conclusions rest on the shaky ground of these narrations of subjective experience. Sartre is, indeed, a brilliant observer of this experience, and his descriptions are worth reading for their psychological insight alone. Nevertheless, as a system of ontology, I do not think it can stand on its own two feet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8867068094409017301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
